Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Definition of Zihar: Body Parts and Mahrams
Defines which comparisons establish zihar and which do not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which verbal comparisons establish zihar according to the Hanafi school and distinguish it from the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali positions on body parts.

Core Idea
Zihar is established only by comparing the wife to a permanently forbidden woman in a part of her body that is haram to look at.

The precise wording of a husband's statement determines whether zihar occurs. The Hanafi school tightly defines zihar as a comparison to a permanently forbidden woman (muharramah ʿalā l-taʾbīd) in a body part that is considered 'awrah — meaning it is impermissible to look at. This lesson unpacks that definition and its application to various body parts and mahrams, as well as the disagreement with the three other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the husband doing? He is uttering a comparison (tashbih). Determine whether this act falls under zihar — a pre-modern form of divorce-like prohibition that requires expiation (kaffarah) before resuming marital relations.
The act itself is haram, but the legal consequences (forbiddance) take effect if the definition is met.
2
Apply the definition of zihar
The Hanafi definition: zihar is a comparison of one's lawful wife (halilah) to a permanently forbidden woman (muharramah) in a part of her body that is haram to look at (ʿuḍw lā yajūzu al-naẓaru ilayhi). Check if the husband's words satisfy all three elements: 1) a mahram woman, 2) a specific body part, 3) that part is 'awrah.
A general comparison like 'you are like my mother' does not specify a body part and thus does not constitute zihar.
3
Distinguish between permissible and impermissible body parts
Hanafi holds that only parts like the belly (baṭn), thigh (fakhidh), and genitals (farj) — the areas of the 'awrah — qualify. Parts like the hand (yad), foot (rijl), neck (ʿunuq), hair (shaʿr), teeth (sinn), and nails (ẓufr) are permissible to look at and do not establish zihar.
This is the point of ikhtilaf with other schools.
4
Extend to other permanently forbidden mahrams
The mother is the primary example, but the same ruling applies to other mahrams who are permanently forbidden: sister (ukht), paternal aunt (ʿammah), foster mother (umm min al-riḍāʿah), etc. They are all in the same category of permanent prohibition.
The comparison must still be to a specific body part that is 'awrah.
5
Evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam Shafiʿi, Malik, and Ahmad hold that comparisons to any part of the mother's body — except those usually not considered shameful (hair, teeth, nails) — establish zihar. The Hanafi response: the effective cause (ʿillah) is the prohibition of looking; hand, foot, neck are not 'awrah, so they do not carry the same connotation.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the marriage is affected in those cases.

Source Text

واذا قال انت على كبطن امى او كفخذها او كفرجها فهو مظاهر لان الظهار ليس الاتشبيه المحللة بالمحرمة وهذا المعنى يتحقق فى عضو لا يجوز النظر اليه
ترجمہ اور اگر کیا تو مجھ پر میری ماں کے شکم کے مانند ہے یا اس کی ران کے مانند ہے، یا اس کی شرمگاہ کے مانند ہے تو یہ شخص ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ظہار تو یہی ہے کہ محلہ کومحرمہ کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی جائے اور یہ معنی ایسے عضو میں متحقق ہو جاتے ہیں جس کی طرف دیکھنا جائز نہیں۔
تشریح مسئلہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت علی کبطن امی یا کہا انست علی کفخد امی یا کہا انت علی کفرج امی - ان تمام صورتوں میں یہ شخص ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا دلیل یہ ہے کہ ظہار کہتے ہیں اپنی منکوحہ کو حرمہ ابدیہ کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا اور یہ معنی ہر ایسے عضو کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینے میں متحقق ہو جائیں گے جس کی طرف دیکھنا نا جائز ہے اور جن اعضاء کی طرف دیکھنا جائز ہے مثلاً ہاتھ ، پاؤں ، بال، ناخن وغیرہ ان کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا ظہار نہ ہوگا اور امام شافعی ، امام مالک اور امام احمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت علی کید امی یا کر جل امی یا کراس امی یا کعنق امی تو یه شخص ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا اور اگر اس کے بال یا دانت یا ناخن کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی ہے تو ان حضرات کے نزدیک بھی ظہار نہیں ہوگا۔
کسی محرمہ کے ساتھ تشبیہ دے دینے سے بھی مظاہر ہوگا
وكذا ان شبهها بمن لا يحل له النظر اليها على التابيد من محارمه مثل اخته او عمته اوامه من الرضاعة لا نهن في التحريم المؤبد كالام
ترجمہ اور اسی طرح (ظہار ہو جائے گا ) اگر بیوی کو اپنے محارم میں سے ایسی عورت کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی کہ اس کو شہوت سے) دیکھنا دائی حرام ہے۔ جیسے اپنی بہن یا اپنی پھوپھی یا رضاعی ماں کیونکہ یہ عورتیں ماں کی طرح دائی حرام ہیں ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو اپنے محارم میں سے ایسی عورت کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی کہ اس کو شہوت کے ساتھ دیکھنا دائی حرام ہے۔ مثلاً بہن، پھوپھی وغیر ہ تو شیخ ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ عورتیں دائی حرام ہونے میں ماں کے مانند ہیں۔ الہذا جو حکم ظہار میں ماں کا ہے وہی ان عورتوں کا ہوگا۔
شوہر نے بیوی کو راسک علی کظہر امی اوفرجک اور جبک اور قتک او نصفک او ٹلسٹک کہا تو مظاہر ہوگا
و كنذالک اذاقال رأسک علی کظهرامی او فرجک او وجھک اور قبتک او نصفک او تلیک لانه
And if he says: 'You are to me like my mother's belly, or like her thigh, or like her private part,' then he is a muzahir (one who performs zihar), because zihar is only the comparison of a lawful wife to a forbidden woman, and this meaning is realized in a part that it is not permissible to look at. (...) And likewise if he compares her to one of his permanently forbidden mahrams, such as his sister, his aunt, or his foster mother, because they are in permanent prohibition like the mother. (...) And likewise when he says: 'Your head is like my mother's back, or your private part, or your face, or your leg, or half of you, or your entire body,' because...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage clarifies the exact wording that constitutes zihar according to the Hanafi school. The key principle is that zihar is a comparison of one's wife to a permanently forbidden woman (like mother) in a part of the body that is haram to view. Thus, comparing to the mother's belly, thigh, or genitals certainly establishes zihar. Comparisons to parts that are permissible to look at (hands, feet, hair, nails) do not establish zihar. The text then records a disagreement with Imam Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad: they hold that comparing to the mother's hand, leg, neck, etc. also constitutes zihar, while they agree that hair, teeth, nails do not. The Hanafi position is based on the 'illa (effective cause): the prohibition applies only to parts that are legally considered 'awrah and haram to look at. The passage further extends the ruling to comparisons with other permanently forbidden mahrams (sister, aunt, foster mother) because they share the same permanent prohibition as the mother. Finally, it mentions additional phrases like 'your head is like my mother's back' and others, indicating that zihar is established by any such comparison to a forbidden body part, even if not the whole body. The final line is cut off, but it likely continues with the reason (la'annahu...).

Key Terms
مظاهر (muzahir): A person who performs zihar, i.e., declares his wife like his mother or other mahram.
تشبيه (tashbih): Comparison; in zihar, it is the act of likening one's wife to a permanently forbidden woman.
محرمة (muharramah): A woman who is permanently forbidden in marriage (like mother, sister, etc.).
عضو لا يجوز النظر إليه (ʿuḍw lā yajūzu al-naẓaru ilayhi): A body part that it is not permissible to look at; i.e., the 'awrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following statements from a husband to his wife would establish zihar?
A) 'Your hand is like my mother's hand'
B) 'Your hair is like my mother's hair'
C) 'Your belly is like my mother's belly'
D) 'You are like my sister'

Key Takeaway
Zihar requires a specific comparison to a permanently forbidden mahram in a body part that is legaly defined as 'awrah (impermissible to look at).

Zihar by Comparing to Specific Body Parts: The 'Awrah Criterion
Ruling that comparing wife to any impermissible-to-look-at body part of a mahram constitutes zihar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zihar when comparing to specific body parts and distinguish it from the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali positions.

Core Idea
In Hanafi, zihar is confirmed only when the wife is likened to a body part that is forbidden to look at.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on zihar when a husband compares his wife to specific body parts of his mother. The matn from Al-Hidayah states that likening to the belly, thigh, or private part constitutes zihar. The commentary explains the defining criterion: the body part must be one that is unlawful to look at (i.e., part of the 'awrah). This leads to a difference with other schools, who extend zihar to more body parts but agree on excluding hair, teeth, and nails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The husband says 'You are to me like my mother's belly/thigh/private part.' According to the Hanafi school, this is a valid zihar declaration, which imposes a suspension of marital relations until kaffarah is performed.
The ruling is from the matn of Al-Hidayah, which is part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the definition of zihar: likening a lawful wife (halal) to a permanently prohibited woman (haram). This meaning is only realized when the body part is one that it is not permissible to look at (i.e., 'awrah).
The commentary explicitly states: 'لان الظهار ليس الا تشبيه المحللة بالمحرمة وهذا المعنى يتحقق فى عضو لا يجوز النظر اليه'.
3
Understand the 'illah
The effective cause (illah) for zihar is the prohibition of looking at the body part. Only those parts that are 'awrah (belly, thigh, private part) carry that prohibition. Permissible parts like hand, foot, hair, nail do not.
This is a linguistic/definitional argument rooted in the Quranic concept of zihar.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that comparing to hand, leg, shin, or neck also constitutes zihar, but hair, tooth, and nail do not. Their basis is that any part of the mother implies the whole, so tashbih is achieved.
The text records their view without rebuttal; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
5
Evaluate the thamarah
The practical difference: If a husband says 'You are like my mother's hand,' in Hanafi it is not zihar, so no kaffarah is needed; in the other schools it is zihar, requiring expiation.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: the same utterance either triggers or does not trigger zihar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شوہر نے بیوی کو کہا انت علی کبطن امی یا کفخذ بایا کفرج جہا کہا یہ مظاہر ہوگا واذا قال انت على كبطن امى او كفخذها او كفرجها فهو مظاهر لان الظهار ليس الاتشبيه المحللة بالمحرمة وهذا المعنى يتحقق فى عضو لا يجوز النظر اليه ترجمہ اور اگر کیا تو مجھ پر میری ماں کے شکم کے مانند ہے یا اس کی ران کے مانند ہے، یا اس کی شرمگاہ کے مانند ہے تو یہ شخص ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ظہار تو یہی ہے کہ محلہ کومحرمہ کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی جائے اور یہ معنی ایسے عضو میں متحقق ہو جاتے ہیں جس کی طرف دیکھنا جائز نہیں۔ تشریح مسئلہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت علی کبطن امی یا کہا انست علی کفخد امی یا کہا انت علی کفرج امی - ان تمام صورتوں میں یہ شخص ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا دلیل یہ ہے کہ ظہار کہتے ہیں اپنی منکوحہ کو حرمہ ابدیہ کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا اور یہ معنی ہر ایسے عضو کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینے میں متحقق ہو جائیں گے جس کی طرف دیکھنا نا جائز ہے اور جن اعضاء کی طرف دیکھنا جائز ہے مثلاً ہاتھ ، پاؤں ، بال، ناخن وغیرہ ان کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا ظہار نہ ہوگا اور امام شافعی ، امام مالک اور امام احمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت علی کید امی یا کر جل امی یا کراس امی یا کعنق امی تو یه شخص ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا اور اگر اس کے بال یا دانت یا ناخن کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی ہے تو ان حضرات کے نزدیک بھی ظہار نہیں ہوگا۔
And if he said, 'You are to me like the belly of my mother' or 'like her thigh' or 'like her private part,' then he is a muzahir, because zihar is nothing but likening a permissible (wife) to a permanently prohibited (woman), and this meaning is verified in a body part that it is not permissible to look at. Translation: And if he said, 'You are to me like the belly of my mother' or 'like her thigh' or 'like her private part,' then this person becomes a muzahir. Explanation: If a husband says to his wife, 'You are to me like the belly of my mother' or 'like the thigh of my mother' or 'like the private part of my mother' – in all these cases he becomes a muzahir. The evidence is that zihar means likening one's wife to a permanently prohibited woman, and this meaning is verified in every body part that it is not permissible to look at. Likening to parts that are permissible to look at, such as hand, foot, hair, nail, etc., does not constitute zihar. Imam al-Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad said: If the husband says to his wife, 'You are to me like the hand of my mother' or 'like the leg of my mother' or 'like the shin of my mother' or 'like the neck of my mother,' then this person becomes a muzahir; but if he likens to her hair, tooth, or nail, then according to them too it is not zihar.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf Ul Hadaya presents the Hanafi ruling on zihar when a husband compares his wife to specific body parts of his mother. The matn (from Al-Hidayah) states that saying 'You are to me like my mother's belly/thigh/private part' constitutes zihar. The commentary explains that zihar is defined as likening a lawful wife to a permanently prohibited woman, and this meaning holds for any body part that is forbidden to look at (i.e., part of the 'awrah). Therefore, permissible body parts like hands, feet, hair, and nails do not trigger zihar. The passage also records the view of the other three Sunni imams who extend zihar to more parts (hand, leg, shin, neck) but agree on excluding hair, teeth, and nails. The underlying proof is the definitional connection between tashbih and the prohibition of looking.

Key Terms
ظہار (zihar): A form of divorce-like declaration by likening one's wife to a permanently forbidden woman (e.g., mother).
تشبیہ (tashbih): Similitude or likening; the essential element in zihar.
محرمہ (mahramah): A woman permanently prohibited in marriage due to blood, fosterage, or marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'You are like my mother's hand'
B) 'You are like my mother's hair'
C) 'You are like my mother's thigh'
D) 'You are like my mother's nail'

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi, zihar is only triggered when a wife is likened to a body part of a mahram that is forbidden to look at, due to the definitional link to permanent prohibition.

Zihar by Comparison to Perpetually Prohibited Mahram
Expansion of zihar ruling to all perpetually prohibited mahram via analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that zihar is established when a husband compares his wife to any perpetually prohibited mahram and explain the analogical reasoning (qiyas) behind extending the mother's ruling.

Core Idea
Zihar applies to all perpetually prohibited mahram, not just the mother, because they share the same illa of tahrim mu'abbad.

In this passage, the Hanafi school expands the scope of zihar beyond the mother to include all women who are permanently forbidden in marriage, such as sisters, aunts, and foster mothers. The key is the perpetual prohibition (tahrim mu'abbad), which forms the basis of the analogy. You will learn how this qiyas works and why temporary prohibitions do not trigger zihar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base case and the new case
The base case is comparing the wife to the mother, which is the original ruling. The new cases are comparing to sister, paternal aunt, or foster mother.
2
Determine the common effective cause (illa)
The illa is perpetual prohibition of marriage (tahrim mu'abbad). Both the mother and the listed women are permanently forbidden to marry.
3
Verify the illa in the new cases
Confirm that sister, paternal aunt, and foster mother share the characteristic of perpetual prohibition. They do.
4
Apply the ruling from base to new
Since the illa is present, the ruling of zihar applies to the new cases as well.
5
Note the boundary: temporary prohibitions excluded
Women who are temporarily prohibited (e.g., sister-in-law) do not share the illa of permanence, so zihar does not occur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی محرمہ کے ساتھ تشبیہ دے دینے سے بھی مظاہر ہوگا وكذا ان شبهها بمن لا يحل له النظر اليها على التابيد من محارمه مثل اخته او عمته اوامه من الرضاعة لا نهن في التحريم المؤبد كالام ترجمہ اور اسی طرح (ظہار ہو جائے گا ) اگر بیوی کو اپنے محارم میں سے ایسی عورت کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی کہ اس کو شہوت سے) دیکھنا دائی حرام ہے۔ جیسے اپنی بہن یا اپنی پھوپھی یا رضاعی ماں کیونکہ یہ عورتیں ماں کی طرح دائی حرام ہیں ۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو اپنے محارم میں سے ایسی عورت کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی کہ اس کو شہوت کے ساتھ دیکھنا دائی حرام ہے۔ مثلاً بہن، پھوپھی وغیر ہ تو شیخ ظہار کرنے والا ہو جائے گا دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ عورتیں دائی حرام ہونے میں ماں کے مانند ہیں۔ الہذا جو حکم ظہار میں ماں کا ہے وہی ان عورتوں کا ہوگا۔
And similarly, zihar occurs if he likens her to one of his mahram whom it is not permissible for him to look at with desire, such as his sister, his paternal aunt, or his foster mother, because they are in perpetual prohibition like the mother. Translation: And similarly (zihar will occur) if he likens his wife to a woman from his mahram whom it is forbidden for him to look at (with desire). Like his sister, his paternal aunt, or his foster mother, because these women are perpetually forbidden like the mother. Explanation: The issue is that if a husband likens his wife to a woman from his mahram whom it is forbidden for him to look at with desire, e.g., sister, paternal aunt, etc., then he becomes a muzahir. The evidence is that these women are like the mother in being perpetually forbidden. Therefore, the same ruling of zihar that applies to the mother applies to these women.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage extends the ruling of zihar beyond the mother to all mahram women who are perpetually prohibited in marriage, such as sister, paternal aunt, and foster mother. The commentary explains that the underlying reason ('illa) is the perpetual prohibition (tahrim mu'abbad). Since these women share this characteristic with the mother, they are treated alike in terms of zihar. The proof is an analogy (qiyas) based on the common attribute of perpetual prohibition. This is a straightforward extension of the ruling from the mother to other mahram.

Key Terms
محارم (mahram): Relatives whom one cannot marry due to blood, fosterage, or marriage affinity.
ظہار (zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a husband declares his wife as forbidden like his mother; in Islamic law, it is a prohibited but legally effective pronouncement requiring expiation.
تحريم مؤبد (tahrim mu'abbad): Perpetual prohibition of marriage, which never becomes permissible (e.g., blood relatives, foster mother, mother-in-law).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements is true according to this passage?
A) Zihar only occurs if the wife is compared to the mother.
B) Zihar occurs if the wife is compared to any permanently prohibited mahram.
C) Zihar occurs if the wife is compared to any female relative.
D) Zihar does not occur with foster mother.

Key Takeaway
Zihar is established by comparing the wife to any woman with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, based on the illa of tahrim mu'abbad.

Zihar by comparing part of wife's body to mother's back
Ruling with proof: Zihar by comparing a part of wife's body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that comparing a part of the wife's body to a mahram constitutes zihar and explain the Hanafi reasoning using the principle of juz' shayi'.

Core Idea
In zihar, comparing a part equals comparing the whole because a part represents the whole.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from Al-Hidayah: a husband who compares any part of his wife's body (e.g., head, face) to his mother's back has performed zihar. The commentary clarifies the legal basis: these body parts are idiomatic expressions for the whole person, and the ruling is established through the principle of juz' shayi' (undifferentiated part). Understanding this helps you apply zihar rules accurately in cases where the husband's wording is partial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act ruled upon
The passage deals with a husband saying 'your head is like my mother's back' and similar expressions. Determine what legal act is being identified.
The act is the utterance (qawl) of the husband.
2
State the ruling
According to the Hanafi school, such utterances constitute zihar. That means the wife becomes prohibited to the husband until he performs kaffarah.
The ruling is that zihar is established (thabit).
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The commentary gives the reason: these body parts are used idiomatically to express the whole body (yu'baru biha 'an jami' al-badan). Find that phrase in the text.
The proof is linguistic custom and analogy with divorce.
4
Explain the reasoning (juz' shayi')
The principle of juz' shayi' means that a representative part carries the ruling of the whole. The ruling is established first in the part (e.g., the head) and then spreads (yata'adda) to the entire body.
This principle is also used in inheritance and divorce.
5
Apply to various body parts
The text lists head, face, private part, neck, half, and third. Consider why half and third are included—they are also juz' shayi'.
Any part that can be considered representative of the whole triggers zihar.

And similarly if he said: 'Your head is like the back of my mother' or 'your private part' or 'your face' or 'your neck' or 'your half' or 'your third,' then he is a muzahir, because these limbs are used to express the whole body, and the ruling is established in the undifferentiated part (shayi') and then spreads, as we explained in divorce. Explanation: If a husband says: 'Your head is like the back of my mother' or 'your private part is like the back of my mother' or 'your face is like the back of my mother' or 'your neck is like the back of my mother' or 'your half is like the back of my mother' or 'your third is like the back of my mother' – in all these cases zihar is established. The evidence is that each of these limbs expresses the whole body, so likening the wife's limb is like likening the wife herself. Therefore, he counts as a muzahir. The ruling is first established in the undifferentiated part (shayi'), then spreads to the entire body. So here too, the ruling of zihar is first established in the wife's half or third, then spreads to the whole body.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage addresses cases where the husband compares a part of his wife's body (like her head, face, neck, or half/third of her body) to his mother's back. The matn from Al-Hidayah rules that this also constitutes zihar. The commentary provides the reasoning: these body parts are used idiomatically to refer to the entire body (jami' al-badan). The ruling is first established in the 'undifferentiated part' (juz' shayi')—a part that represents the whole—and then spreads (yata'adda) to the entire body. This is analogous to the rule in divorce, where naming a part implies the whole. Therefore, saying 'your head is like my mother's back' results in zihar for the entire wife.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' shayi'): An undifferentiated part that is representative of the whole; used in inheritance, divorce, and here in zihar to spread the ruling from a part to the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the head is mentioned in the Quran for zihar.
C) Because such parts are used idiomatically to express the whole body.
D) Because the husband explicitly meant the whole body.

Key Takeaway
Comparing any part of the wife's body to a mahram's back constitutes zihar because that part represents the whole body (juz' shayi').

Intra-Hanafi dispute on saying 'You are like my mother' without additional words
Intra-Hanafi dispute on default ruling of ambiguous tashbih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the default ruling of the phrase 'You are like my mother' when no intention is present, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Without intention, 'You are like my mother' is just honor — unless you are Imam Muhammad.

This passage examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal effect of a husband saying to his wife '"You are like my mother"' without any additional words or expressed intention. While the phrase could imply zihar, divorce, or honor, the key disagreement is about the default ruling when the husband has no intention at all. The passage clarifies the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expression as kinayah
Recognize that the phrase '"You are like my mother"' does not explicitly mention 'zahr' (back), so it is not sarih for zihar. It is kinayah (ambiguous), requiring intention for legal effect.
In Hanafi, only words containing 'zahr' are sarih for zihar.
2
State the ruling when intention is present
The passage lists three possible intentions: honor (karamah) -> no effect; zihar -> becomes zihar; divorce -> becomes talaq ba'in.
Each intention triggers a different legal outcome.
3
Contrast the two views on absence of intention
Compare the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: it is nothing) with Muhammad's view (it is zihar by qiyas awlawi).
This is the core ikhtilaf.
4
Trace the proof and thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the dalil for each side: majority relies on the possibility of honor (ihtimal al-haml 'ala al-karamah); Muhammad uses a fortiori analogy. The practical difference is whether zihar is established.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text

شوہر کا باندی کو انت علی مثل امی او کامی کہنے کا حکم ولو قال انت على مثل امى او كامى یرجع الی نیته لينكشف حكمه فان قال اردت الكرامة فهو كما قال لان التكريم بالتشبيه فاش فى الكلام و ان قال اردت الظهار فهو ظهار لانه تشبيه بجميعها وفيه تشبيه بالعضو لكنه ليس بصريح فيفتقر الى النية وان قال اردت الطلاق فهو طلاق بائن لانه تشبيه بالام في الحرمة فكانه قال انت على حرام ونوى الطلاق وان لم يكن له نية فليس بشيء عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف لاحتمال الحمل على الكرامة وقال محمد يكون ظهارا لان التشبيه بعضومنها لما كان ظهارا فالتشبيه بجميعها اولى وان عنى به التحريم لاغير فعند ابي يوسف هو ايلاء ليكون الثابت به ادنى الحرمتين و عند محمد ظهارلان كاف التشبيه تختص ترجمہ اور اگر شوہر نے کہا کہ تو مجھ پر میری ماں کے مثل ہے یا میری ماں کے مانند ہے تو اس کی نیت کی طرف رجوع کیا جائے گا تا کہ اس کا حکم ظاہر ہو پس اگر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے کرامت کا ارادہ کیا ہے تو یہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ اس نے کہا۔ کیونکہ تشبیہ کے ذریعہ اکرام کرنا کلام میں بہت مروج ہے اور اگر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے ظہار کا ارادہ کیا ہے تو یہ ظہار ہے کیونکہ یہ ماں کے پورے بدن سے تشبیہ ہے اور اس میں عضو کے ساتھ تشبیہ بھی موجود ہے لیکن یہ صریح نہیں اس لئے نیت کی طرف محتاج ہو گا اور اگر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے طلاق کا ارادہ کیا ہے تو یہ طلاق بائن ہے کیونکہ یہ حرام ہونے میں ماں کے ساتھ تشبیہ ہے گویا اس نے انت علی حرام کہا اور طلاق کی نیت کی اور اگر اس کی کچھ نیت نہ ہو تو ابو حنیفہ اور ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یہ کلام کچھ نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ کرامت پر محمول کرنے کا احتمال ہے اور امام محمد نے فرمایا کہ ظہار ہوگا کیونکہ (بیوی کو ) جب ماں کے ایک عضو کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا ظہار ہو جاتا ہے تو ماں کے پورے بدن سے تشبیہ دینا بدرجہ اولی ظہار ہوگا اور اگر اس نے اس کلام سے فقط حرام کرنا مراد لیا ہے تو ابو یوسف کے نزدیک یہ ایلاء ہے تا کہ اس کلام سے دو حرمتوں میں سے اونی
And if he said: 'You are like my mother' or 'like my mother,' he is referred to his intention to reveal its ruling. If he said, 'I intended honor,' then it is as he said, because honoring by likening is widespread in speech. And if he said, 'I intended zihar,' then it is zihar, because it is likening to her entire body, and it contains likening to a part, but it is not explicit (sarih), so it requires intention. And if he said, 'I intended divorce,' then it is an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in), because it is likening to the mother in prohibition, as if he said 'You are forbidden to me' and intended divorce. And if he had no intention, then according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf it is nothing, because it can be interpreted as honor. Imam Muhammad said: It is zihar, because likening to a part of the mother is zihar, so likening to all of her is even more so. And if he meant only prohibition (tahrim) with nothing else, then according to Abu Yusuf it is ila' (an oath of abstinence), so that the lesser of the two prohibitions is established; according to Muhammad it is zihar, because the particle of comparison (kaf al-tashbih) is specific...
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage discusses the phrase 'You are like my mother' (without specifying a body part). The ruling is that it is not explicit (sarih) for zihar, so it depends on the husband's intention. He may intend honor (karamah), in which case it is nothing; zihar, in which case it becomes zihar; or divorce, in which case it becomes talaq ba'in. The intra-Hanafi dispute concerns the default ruling when the husband has no intention. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that it is nothing (laysa bi shay') because it can be interpreted as honor. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that it is zihar, arguing by a fortiori reasoning: if likening to a part of the mother is zihar, then likening to her entire body is even more so. Additionally, if the husband intends only prohibition (tahrim) without specifying zihar or divorce, Abu Yusuf rules it is ila' (an oath of abstaining from intercourse) to apply the lesser prohibition, while Muhammad rules it is zihar because the particle of comparison (kaf al-tashbih) specifically indicates zihar. This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the interpretation of ambiguous language.

Key Terms
صریح (sarih): Explicit, unambiguous; used here to describe words that directly indicate zihar without need for intention.
کنایہ (kinayah): Ambiguous or indirect expression that requires intention (niyyah) to become effective.
ایلاء (ila'): An oath to abstain from marital relations for four months; here Abu Yusuf interprets the phrase as ila' when only tahrim is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute about saying 'You are like my mother' without any intention, what is the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?
A) It is zihar.
B) It is nothing (just honor).
C) It is talaq.
D) It is ila'.

Key Takeaway
Without intention, the phrase 'You are like my mother' has no legal effect according to the majority of the Hanafi school; Imam Muhammad's contrary view is based on a fortiori reasoning but is not the relied-upon position.

Zihar Established with Any Body Part
Establishing zihar when husband compares body parts like head, face, half to mother.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that zihar is established when the husband compares any body part of his wife to his mother's back, and explain the Hanafi reasoning (qiyas) that the part represents the whole.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, likening any part of the wife to the mother's back constitutes zihar, as the part represents the whole body.

Zihar is a pre-Islamic form of divorce that Islam reformed. This lesson examines a specific ruling: does zihar occur when the husband compares only a body part (e.g., head, face, half) of his wife to his mother's back? The Hanafi school answers affirmatively, based on the principle that such parts are conventionally used to refer to the whole body. We will explore the textual basis and analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband's statement comparing a body part of his wife to his mother's back. The ruling is that zihar is established (sahih).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to the rule in divorce where a part represents the whole. The text states that these limbs express the entire body.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'يعبر بها عن جميع البدن' indicates that each limb is used metonymically for the body. So comparing a limb is like comparing the whole.
This is the key to understanding the extension.
4
Examine the extension
The ruling first applies to the common part (al-juz' al-shā'i') and then spreads to the entire body.
This is analogous to the rule in talaq.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school may require mention of the whole body or specific parts like the back. The Hanafi position is more inclusive.

Source Text

اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد به شم

Zihar. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary of Hidaya, volume 5. (It is expressed by all of the body, and the ruling is established in the common part, then it extends, as we explained in divorce). Translation: Similarly, if he said: 'Your head on my mother's back' or 'your hair' or 'your body' or 'your neck' or 'your half (body)' or 'your third' because these limbs express the entire body, and the ruling is established in the common part then it spreads. Issue: If the husband said to his wife: 'Your head on my mother's back' or 'your private part on my mother's back' or 'your face on my mother's back' or 'your neck on my mother's back' or 'your half on my mother's back' or 'your portion on my mother's back' – in all these cases zihar is established. The proof is that each of these limbs expresses the whole body, so comparing the wife's limbs is like comparing the wife herself. Therefore this person is considered a zihar-doer, and the ruling first establishes in the common part then extends to the entire body.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on zihar when the husband compares a specific body part of his wife to his mother's back. The author states that if the husband says 'your head on my mother's back' or similar phrases mentioning parts like hair, body, neck, half, or third, zihar is established. The reasoning (dalil) is that these limbs represent the entire body, so the ruling first applies to the common part (juz' shai') then extends to the whole. This is analogous to the rule in divorce (talaq) as previously explained. The text emphasizes that the ruling spreads from the part to the whole, so t hat the wife becomes forbidden to the husband until he performs expiation. This is a standard Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الجزء الشائع (al-juz' al-shā'i'): The common part or undivided share; here it refers to the specific body part mentioned (e.g., head) which represents the whole body.
ظهار (zihar): A pre-Islamic form of divorce where a husband compares his wife to his mother's back, resulting in prohibition until expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, zihar is established by comparing any body part of the wife to a mahram's back, as the part legally represents the whole.

Ruling on 'Anti 'ala Mithli Ummi' and the Role of Intention
Explaining the effect of saying 'you are like my mother' depending on the husband's intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the phrase 'anti 'ala mithli ummi' and apply the requirement of intention to determine the ruling.

Core Idea
Without intention, the phrase is ambiguous — the Hanafi majority rules it void, but Muhammad says it is zihar.

This passage from al-Hidayah explores the legal effect of a husband saying to his wife 'you are like my mother'. The ruling hinges on his intention: honour, zihar, divorce, or mere prohibition. The core dispute among the Hanafi imams arises when no intention is expressed. Understanding this ambiguity clarifies the necessity of niyyah in non-explicit statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its ambiguity
The phrase 'anti 'ala mithli ummi' or 'ka ummi' compares the wife to the husband's mother. This comparison can be interpreted in multiple ways: as honour, as a declaration of zihar, as a form of divorce, or as a mere prohibition. The ambiguity requires intention to resolve.
2
Understand the role of intention
The husband's stated intention determines the legal ruling. If he intends honour, it is honour; if he intends zihar, it is zihar; if he intends divorce, it is a ba'in divorce; if he intends only prohibition, there is disagreement.
The text emphasizes that the phrase is not explicit (sarih) for any one ruling, so intention is needed.
3
List the possible intentions and resulting rulings
Honour: no legal effect. Zihar: requires expiation. Divorce: ba'in talaq. Prohibition only: Abu Yusuf says it becomes ila', Muhammad says zihar. No intention: majority Hanafi (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf) say nothing; Muhammad says zihar.
4
Focus on the no-intention scenario
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that without intention, the phrase is meaningless because it can be interpreted as honour. Muhammad argues that comparison with the whole (mother) is more worthy than comparison with a part, so it must be zihar. The majority view is the mufta bihi position.
The dispute is based on whether the phrase is ambiguous enough to allow an honourable interpretation.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, if a husband says this without intention, a Hanafi mufti following the majority would rule no effect, while a Shafi'i or Hanafi following Muhammad would rule zihar. This affects whether the husband must perform kaffarah before resuming marital relations.

کو کھانا کھلانا ہے۔ (معینی شرح ہدایہ)
And if he said: 'You are like my mother' or 'like my mother', he is referred to his intention to clarify his ruling. So if he said: 'I intended honour', it is as he said, because honouring by comparison is common in speech. And if he said: 'I intended zihar', it is zihar because it is a comparison with her entire body, and it contains comparison with a limb, but it is not explicit, so it requires intention. And if he said: 'I intended divorce', it is a ba'in divorce because it is a comparison with the mother in prohibition, as if he said 'you are prohibited' and intended divorce. And if he had no intention, abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say it is nothing, because it can be interpreted as honour. Muhammad said it becomes zihar because comparison with a part constitutes zihar, so comparison with the whole is more appropriate. And if he meant only prohibition without more, according to Abu Yusuf it is ila' so that the lesser of the two prohibitions is established, and according to Muhammad it is zihar because the 'ka' of comparison is specific to zihar. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary... (Translation continues with detailed explanation of intentions, disputes, and evidence).
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'you are like my mother' (anti 'ala mithli ummi or ka ummi). The text explains that the husband's intention determines the ruling: if he intends honour, it is honour (no legal effect); if he intends zihar, it is zihar; if he intends divorce, it is a ba'in divorce. The key dispute is when there is no intention: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it is meaningless (nothing), while Imam Muhammad holds it is zihar by default. The passage also discusses if the husband intends mere prohibition (without specifying): Abu Yusuf says it becomes ila' (an oath of abstinence), while Muhammad says it is zihar because the comparison is specific to zihar. The commentary then gives reasons for Abu Yusuf's view that ila' is a lesser prohibition than zihar. The text also mentions positions of other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Ahmad) and provides proofs for each view. This is a classic example of textual ambiguity requiring niyyah (intention) to resolve.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihār): A form of divorce-like prohibition where a husband compares his wife to a forbidden female relative (like mother), requiring expiation before resuming marital relations.
إيلاء (īlā'): An oath of abstinence from marital intercourse for four months or more.
كاف التشبيه (kāf al-tashbīh): The letter 'ka' used for comparison, as in 'ka'ummi' (like my mother).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if a husband says 'anti 'ala mithli ummi' without any intention, what is the ruling?
A) It is zihar.
B) It is a ba'in divorce.
C) It is nothing (no legal effect).
D) It is ila'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that without intention, the ambiguous phrase 'anti 'ala mithli ummi' has no legal effect.

The Ruling on 'Anti 'Alayya Ka'umi' and the Role of Intention
Explains ruling when husband states intention after saying 'anti 'alayya ka'umi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three possible rulings for the statement 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' based on the husband's intention and apply the Hanafi qiyas for zihar.

Core Idea
The ruling of 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' depends entirely on the husband's intention: respect, zihar, or talaq.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common ambiguous expression in marital speech. The husband says to his wife 'you are like my mother' – but what does he mean? The Hanafi school teaches that we must ask his intention because the phrase can denote respect, zihar (a form of prohibition requiring expiation), or even talaq. Understanding how to determine the ruling is crucial for issuing correct fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous statement
The phrase 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' (you are like my mother) can imply different meanings. The husband's intention is the key factor.
2
Analyze the intention of respect
If the husband says he intended respect or honor, his speech is interpreted accordingly and nothing is wajib upon him. Proof: common usage where people compare elders to parents out of respect.
This is based on the principle that kinayah words are interpreted by intention.
3
Analyze the intention of zihar
If he intended zihar, the statement becomes zihar. The proof is qiyas jali: comparing to the entire mother is stronger than comparing to a limb, which already establishes zihar.
See the qiyas panel for details.
4
Analyze the intention of talaq
If he intended talaq, talaq bain occurs. The proof is that the phrase implies unlawfulness (haram) similar to 'anti 'alayya haram', which is kinayah for talaq.
Talaq bain is irrevocable and requires a new marriage to reunite.
5
Confirm the Hanafi approach
The school consistently relies on intention for ambiguous expressions. This passage illustrates how intention determines the ruling.

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا انت علی کامی یا انست علی مثل امی تو اس شخص کی نیست دریافت کی جائے گی تا کہ اس کا حکم ظہار ہو یعنی جیسی نیست بیان کرے گا ویسا ہی حکم ہو گا یہی قول امام شافعی کا ہے اور نیت اس لئے معلوم کی جائے گی کہ اس کا کلام تشبیہ کی چند صورتوں کا احتمال رکھتا ہے پس ایک صورت کو متعین کرنے کے لئے نیت کا پایا جانا ضروری ہے پس اگر اس شخص نے کہا کہ میرا ارادہ تو یہ تھا کہ تو سحق اکرام ہونے میں میرے نزدیک میری ماں کے مانند ہے تو یہ ایسا ہی ہے جیسا کہ اس نے کہا یعنی اس کلام کو اس کی نیت پر محمول کیا جائے گا اور اس شخص پر کچھ واجب نہ ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ تشبیہ کے ذریعہ تعظیم و تکریم کلام میں بہت مروج ہے جیسے لوگ کسی بڑے بزرگ کو دیکھ کر کہتے ہیں آپ تو میرے باپ کے مرتبہ میں ہمیں اس کلام سے اس شخص کی مراد یہ ہے کہ میرے دل میں آپ کی توقیر میرے باپ کی طرح ہے حاصل یہ ہے کہ اس ں نے اپنے کلام کے محتمل کی نیت کی ہے اس وجہ سے اس کی نیت صحیح ہوگی۔ اور اگر اس نے کہا کہ میں نے اپنے کلام سے ظہار کا ارادہ کیا ہے تو اس کا یہ کلام ظہار ہو جائے گا دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس شخص نے اپنے کا ام انت على مثل امی سے اپنی بیوی کو اپنی پوری ماں کے ساتھ تشبیہ دی ہے پس جب ماں کے ایک عضو کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینے سے ظہار ہو جاتا ہے تو پوری ماں کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینے سے بدرجہ اولی ظہار ہو جائے گا اور چونکہ انت علی مثل امی میں عضو کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا موجود ہے مگر صریح نہیں اس لئے نیت کی طرف محتاج ہوگا اور اگر شوہر نے کہا کہ میں نے اس کلام سے طلاق کی نیت کی ہے تو طلاق بائن واقع ہو جائے گی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ کلام یعنی است علی مثل امی حرام ہونے میں ماں کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا ہے گویا شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے انت علی حرام کہا ہے اور طلاق کی نیت کی ہے اور سابق میں گزر چکا ہے کہ انت علی حرام طلاق کے الفاظ کنایہ میں سے ہے اور لفظ کنایہ سے طلاق بائن واقع ہوتی ہے بشرطیکہ نیت کی ہو اس وجہ سے اس صورت میں اگر طلاق کا ارادہ کیا ہے تو طلاق بائن واقع ہو جائے گی
Explanation of the case: If someone said to his wife 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' or 'anti 'alayya mithl ummi', then his intention will be asked so that its ruling becomes zihar, i.e. as he states his intention, so will be the ruling. This is the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i. Intention is asked because his speech is capable of several forms of comparison, so to specify one form, having intention is necessary. If the person said 'My intention was that you are like my mother in terms of honor and respect in my view', then it is as he said, i.e. his speech will be interpreted according to his intention, and nothing is wajib upon him. The proof is that using comparison for honor and respect is very common in speech, like people say to a great elder 'You are like my father in status'. From this speech, the person's intent is that your honor in my heart is like my father's. The outcome is that he intended the possible meaning of his speech, so his intention is correct. And if he said 'I intended zihar from my speech', then his speech becomes zihar. The proof is that this person compared his wife to his entire mother with his words 'anti 'alayya mithl ummi'. Since comparing to one limb of the mother makes zihar, comparing to the entire mother a fortiori makes zihar. And since in 'anti 'alayya mithl ummi' the comparison to a limb is present but not explicit, it requires intention. And if the husband said 'I intended talaq from this speech', then talaq bain occurs. The proof is that this speech 'anti 'alayya mithl ummi' involves comparison to the mother in being unlawful (haram), as if he said 'anti 'alayya haram' to his wife and intended talaq. It has previously passed that 'anti 'alayya haram' is among the words of kinayah (allusive divorce), and from kinayah talaq bain occurs provided intention. Therefore, in this case, if he intended talaq, talaq bain occurs.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof. It discusses the statement 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' (you are like my mother) or similar. The author explains that the husband's intention is inquired to determine the legal effect: if he intends respect, nothing is required; if he intends zihar, it is zihar; if he intends talaq, it is talaq bain. Each ruling is supported by a proof. For respect, the proof is common usage. For zihar, qiyas (analogy) is used: comparing to one limb of mother is zihar, so comparing to the whole mother is even more so. For talaq, the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram' is kinayah for talaq, and since talaq is intended, it takes effect. The passage emphasizes the importance of intention in ambiguous expressions.

Key Terms
ظہار (zihar): A form of divorce-like statement where a husband compares his wife to a female relative in a prohibited degree, requiring expiation before resuming relations.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that severs the marriage immediately without possibility of revocation, requiring a new marriage to reunite.
کنایہ (kinayah): Allusive words that can imply divorce but are not explicit, requiring intention to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' and intends respect, what is the ruling?
A) It becomes zihar
B) It becomes talaq bain
C) Nothing is wajib upon him
D) It becomes ila'

Key Takeaway
The legal effect of an ambiguous expression like 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' is determined by the speaker's intention, which must be sought to issue the correct ruling.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Ila' or Zihar Without Intention
Intra-Hanafi dispute with proofs, comparing severity of ila' and zihar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the statement 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' without intention, list the five differences between ila' and zihar cited by Abu Yusuf, and apply the principle of interpreting the lesser prohibition.

Core Idea
When a statement can be interpreted as either ila' or zihar, the lesser prohibition (ila') is chosen – unless the wording is specific to zihar.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about a husband's statement 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' when he has no specific intention. Scholars differ on whether this constitutes ila' (an oath of abstinence) or zihar (a forbidden comparison). Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school weighs textual clues and the severity of prohibitions to resolve ambiguous cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two views
Read the passage to see that without intention, Abu Yusuf says it is ila', while Muhammad says it is zihar. Other schools have their own positions.
The text also mentions that Ahmad, Shafi'i, and Malik consider it zihar by a fortiori analogy.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's five differences
List the five reasons Abu Yusuf gives to show that ila' is less severe than zihar: (1) prohibition timing, (2) ability to remove, (3) moral nature, (4) expiation severity, (5) cause of prohibition.
These differences support his principle: when two prohibitions combine, interpret as the lesser.
3
Analyze Muhammad's counter-argument
Muhammad focuses on the word 'ka' (comparison letter), which is specific to zihar according to Hanafi usage. Therefore, the speech is zihar regardless of intent.
This textual specificity overrides the general principle of choosing the lesser prohibition.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
If ila', the husband must either resume intercourse within four months or divorce; if zihar, he must expiate before any intercourse.
The practical difference is significant: one involves a waiting period, the other an immediate expiation.
5
Note the absence of tarjih
The passage ends with 'Allah knows best', indicating no definitive preference between the two Hanafi positions. Both are transmitted and operative in different contexts.
This reflects the richness of the Hanafi school – multiple views coexist.

اور اگر اس شخص نے کوئی نیت نہیں کی ہے تو یہ کلام شیخین کے نزدیک لغو ہو گا اسی کے قائل اصحاب شافعی ہیں۔ دلیل مجھے کہ یہ کلام مجمل ہے اور متکلم نے اپنی مراد بیان نہیں کی ہے اس وجہ سے اس کا مصداق متعین نہیں کیا جا سکتا کیونکہ اگر یہ کلام طلاق اور ظہار کا احتمال رکھتا ہے تو اس میں کرامت کا احتمال بھی موجود ہے اور امام احمد نے فرمایا ہے کہ عدم نیت کی صورت میں یہ کلام ظہار ہوگا اور اسی کے قائل امام شافعی ، امام مالک اور امام احمد ہیں ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب ماں کے ایک عضو کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا ظہار ہے تو پوری ماں کے ساتھ تشبیہ دینا بدرجہ اولیٰ ظہار ہوگا۔ امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ چند وجوہ سے حرمت ظہار کے مقابلہ میں حرمت ایلا وادنی ہے۔ 1) حرمت ایلاء بالفعل ثابت نہیں ہوتی بلکہ چار ماہ گزر جانے کے بعد ثابت ہوتی ہے اور ظہار میں حرمت بالفعل ثابت ہو جاتی ہے۔ (۲) مدت ایلا میں وطی کے ذریعہ حرمت ایلاء کا دور کرناممکن ہے اس کے برخلاف ظہار ہے کہ اس میں کفارہ دینے سے پہلے وطی کرنا نا جائز ہے۔ ۳) ظہار منکر امن القول وزور ہے یعنی بخش اور جھوٹ بات ہے اور ایلاء مباح اور جائز ہے۔ (۴ ایلاء کا کفارہ تین روزے ہیں اور ظہار کا کفارہ ساٹھ روزے ہیں ۔ یا ایلاء کا کفارہ دس مسکینوں کو کھانا کھلانا ہے اور ظہار کا کفارہ ساٹھے مسکینوں کو کھانا کھلانا ہے۔ (معینی شرح ہدایہ) 
باب الظهار
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد پنجم (۵) ایلا کی حرمت لغیر ہا ہے، یعنی اللہ کے نام کی بے حرمتی کرنے کی وجہ سے حرمت پیدا ہوتی ہے اور ظہار کی حرمت لعینہا ہے کیوں کہ ظہار منکر امن القول وزورا ہے۔ (عنایہ ) حاصل یہ ہے کہ ایلاء کی حرمت اونی اور اخف ہے بمقابلہ حرمت ظہار کے اور قائدہ ہے کہ کسی کلام میں دوحرمتیں جمع ہو جائیں تو اس کلام کو ادنی درجہ کی حرمت پر محمول کیا جائے گا اس لئے انت علی مثل امی کو تحریم کا ارادہ کرنے کی صورت میں حرمت ایلاء پر محمول کیا جائے گا نہ کہ حرمت ظہار پر ۔ اور امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ انت علی مثل امی اور کامی میں حرف تشبیہ موجود ہے اور حرف تشبیہ اور کاف تشبیہ ظہار کے ساتھ مخصوص ہے اس لئے اس کلام کو ظہار کے معنی پر محمول کیا جائے گا۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب
And if the person had no intention, then this speech according to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?) is void (laghw), and the supporters of Shafi'i also say this. The proof is that this speech is mujmal (ambiguous) and the speaker did not express his intent, so its referent cannot be determined. If it could indicate talaq and zihar, it could also indicate honor. Imam Ahmad said that in case of no intention, the speech is zihar, and so also Imam Shafi'i, Imam Malik, and Imam Ahmad. The proof is that comparing to one limb of mother is zihar, so comparing to the whole mother is a fortiori zihar. Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that for several reasons the prohibition of ila' is lesser than that of zihar: 1) The prohibition of ila' is not established immediately but after four months, while zihar's prohibition is immediate. 2) In ila', the prohibition can be removed by intercourse during the period, while in zihar, intercourse before expiation is impermissible. 3) Zihar is munkar min al-qawl wa zour (evil and false speech), while ila' is permissible and lawful. 4) The expiation for ila' is three fasts, while for zihar it is sixty fasts; or feeding ten poor for ila' vs. feeding sixty poor for zihar. (From Ma'ini Sharh Hidayah) Chapter of Zihar. Ashraf al-Hidayah Sharh Urdu Hidayah Volume 5. 5) The prohibition of ila' is li-ghayrihi (due to something else, i.e., swearing by Allah's name), while the prohibition of zihar is li-'aynihi (inherent). So the prohibition of ila' is lesser and lighter than that of zihar. The principle is that when two prohibitions combine in one statement, it is interpreted according to the lesser. Therefore, 'anti 'alayya mithl ummi' when intending prohibition is interpreted as ila', not zihar. Imam Muhammad's proof is that 'anti 'alayya mithl ummi' and 'ka'umi' contain a letter of comparison, and the letter of comparison (ka) is specific to zihar, so the speech is interpreted as zihar. Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the statement 'anti 'alayya ka'umi' when the husband has no specific intention. The text first mentions that according to 'Shaykhayn' (likely Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) it is considered void (laghw) because it is ambiguous, and some other schools agree. It then presents the views of Ahmad, Shafi'i, and Malik that it is zihar based on the analogy that a comparison to the whole mother is stronger than to a limb. The main dispute is between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf provides five differences showing that ila' is less severe than zihar: (1) timing of prohibition, (2) ability to remove prohibition, (3) moral nature, (4) expiation severity, (5) cause of prohibition. Therefore, when a statement can be interpreted as either, it should be taken as the lesser (ila'). Muhammad argues that the presence of the comparison word (ka/ka'umi) is specific to zihar, so it must be zihar. The text does not give a definitive resolution but presents both wajh.

Key Terms
ایلاء (ila'): An oath of abstinence from marital intercourse, usually by swearing by Allah, resulting in a four-month waiting period.
ظہار (zihar): A statement likening the wife to a prohibited relative, requiring expiation.
حرمت (hurmah): Prohibition or sanctity; used comparatively to indicate severity of prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many differences between ila' and zihar does Abu Yusuf cite to argue that ila' is less severe?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when a statement can be interpreted as either ila' or zihar, the prevailing principle is to adopt the lesser prohibition (ila'), but the presence of a comparison word may tilt it to zihar.

Ruling on 'Anti 'Alayya Haram Ka'Umi' with and without Intention
Explains the general principle and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'ka'umi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a husband who says 'anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' with intention for Zihar or talaq, and without intention, identifying the positions of Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
When 'ka'umi' is used without intention, Abu Yusuf rules Ila', Muhammad rules Zihar.

The Hanafi school engages in a nuanced intra-madhhab dispute over ambiguous statements that could indicate Zihar, Ila', or talaq. This lesson unpacks the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' (you are forbidden to me like my mother) and how the Sahibayn differ when the husband's intention is absent or specific. The key lies in the interplay between the particle of comparison (ka) and the ambiguous term 'haram'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the situation
The husband says 'anti 'alayya haram ka'umi'. Determine whether he had an explicit intention (Zihar, talaq, or nothing).
The text distinguishes three scenarios: intent for Zihar, intent for talaq, or no intent.
2
Understand the two possible legal consequences
Zihar is a prohibited comparison requiring expiation (kaffarah); Ila' is an oath of abstinence with a four-month waiting period. Both are forms of prohibition but differ in severity and remedy.
The principle from the text: when two prohibitions combine, the lighter applies.
3
Apply the rule for explicit intention
If the husband intends Zihar or talaq, the statement is interpreted according to his intention because the phrase is ambiguous (yuh'tamil al-wajhain).
Both Sahibayn agree on this point; the ikhtilaf arises only when there is no intention.
4
Analyze the case of no intention
Without intention, Abu Yusuf holds it is Ila' (lighter), while Muhammad holds it is Zihar (due to the particle of comparison being specific to Zihar).
Abu Yusuf relies on the principle that ambiguous prohibition is interpreted as the lighter; Muhammad relies on the explicit nature of tashbih.
5
Evaluate the proofs
Abu Yusuf: 'Haram' is ambiguous and should be the lighter (Ila'). Muhammad: 'Ka' is a particle of comparison which is sarih for Zihar, overriding the ambiguity.
The text leaves both views without explicit tarjih.

ہو جائے گا۔
امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے انت علی حرام کہا اور طلاق کی نیت کی تو طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی اس کے بعد کظہر امی سے ظہار نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ بینونت کے بعد ظہار صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ اور امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اس کی نیت سے اور کنظبر می ، ظہار کے معنی میں صریح ہے۔ لہذا ی شخص ظہار کرنے والا بھی شمار ہو گا اور کلام کو اس کے ظاہر سے پھیرنے میں اس کی تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی۔ امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ کظہر امی ظہار کے معنی میں صریح ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ ظہار پر دلالت کرنے میں یہ لفظ نیت کا محتاج نہیں ہے پس اس لفظ میں ظہار کے علاوہ طلاق اور ایلاء کا احتمال نہیں ہو سکتا۔ پھر لفظ کظہر امی عدم احتمال غیر کی وجہ سے ظہار کے معنی میں محکم ہے اور انت علی حرام کئی معنی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے اور قاعدہ ہے کہ حمل کو محکم کی طرف پھیر دیا جاتا ہے اس وجہ سے انت علی حرام کو بھی ظہار کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا۔
Chapter of Zihar. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary on Hidaya, volume 5. Ila's prohibitory effect is due to something external (ghayriha), meaning prohibition arises due to disrespecting Allah's name, while Zihar's prohibition is intrinsic (li-'aynihi) because Zihar is 'an abhorrent saying and a lie' (from the Quran). The outcome is that Ila's prohibition is lesser and lighter compared to Zihar's prohibition. And the principle is that if two prohibitions are combined in a single statement, that statement will be interpreted in accordance with the lesser prohibition. Therefore, when one says 'You are to me like my mother' with the intent of prohibition, it is interpreted as Ila, not Zihar. Imam Muhammad's proof is that in 'anti 'ala mithli ummi' there is the particle of comparison (harf al-tashbih), and the particle of comparison is specific to Zihar, so this statement is interpreted in the meaning of Zihar. And Allah knows best. A husband says to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' and intends Zihar or divorce; it is according to his intention. And if he says 'Anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' and intends Zihar or divorce, it is as he intended, because it can bear both meanings: Zihar due to the presence of comparison, and divorce due to the prohibition, and the comparison is a confirmation of the prohibition. If he has no intention, then according to Abu Yusuf it is Ila, and according to Muhammad it is Zihar. The two views have been explained. Commentary: If a husband says to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' and intends Zihar or divorce, then this statement is according to his intention. The proof is that this statement can bear both Zihar and divorce. Zihar because comparison is found, divorce because he has made her forbidden to himself, and in this case the comparison is a confirmation of the prohibition. If this person did not intend anything by his statement, then according to Imam Abu Yusuf this statement is Ila, and according to Imam Muhammad it is Zihar. The proofs of both have passed in the previous issue. A husband says to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' and intends divorce or Ila; is it Zihar or Ila? The statements of the jurists: If he says 'Anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' and intends by it divorce or Ila, it is not Zihar according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is as he intended, because prohibition can bear all of that as we have explained. However, according to Muhammad, if he intended divorce, it is not Zihar; according to Abu Yusuf, both occur. This has been mentioned in its place. Abu Hanifa's proof is that this statement is explicit (sarih) in the meaning of Zihar, so it cannot bear other than that. Then it is authoritative (muhkam), so this prohibition is referred to the prohibition of Zihar. Commentary: The issue is that a person said to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' and intended divorce or Ila. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this statement is only Zihar, and Imam Ahmad holds the same. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is what he intended: if he intended Zihar, then Zihar; if divorce, then divorce; if Ila, then Ila. The proof of the two is that his statement 'Anti 'alayya haram' can bear each of the mentioned matters, and intention is valid for probability, so the ruling is according to his intention. However, there is a difference between the statements of the two: according to Imam Muhammad, if he intended divorce, only divorce occurs and this statement is not Zihar; according to Imam Abu Yusuf, this statement becomes both divorce and Zihar. Imam Muhammad's proof is that when the husband said 'Anti 'alayya haram' and intended divorce, a ba'in divorce occurs; after that, 'kazahr ummi' does not effect Zihar because Zihar is not valid after separation (baynunah). Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that divorce occurs due to his intention, and 'kazahr ummi' is explicit in the meaning of Zihar, so this person is also considered a mazahir, and his word is not trusted when turning it from its apparent meaning. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the word 'kazahr ummi' is explicit in the meaning of Zihar; that is why it does not need intention in indicating Zihar. So this word cannot bear anything other than Zihar, such as divorce or Ila. Then the word 'kazahr ummi' is authoritative (muhkam) due to its lack of probability for other meanings, and 'Anti 'alayya haram' can bear several meanings. The principle is that interpretation is turned towards the authoritative, so 'Anti 'alayya haram' is also turned towards Zihar.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya discusses the complex rulings surrounding ambiguous statements in the context of Zihar (a pre-Islamic practice prohibited in Islam that requires expiation). The passage first distinguishes between Ila' (an oath of abstinence) and Zihar, noting that Ila's prohibition is lighter because it is based on a secondary cause, whereas Zihar's prohibition is intrinsic. A key principle is established: when a statement can carry two different types of prohibition, it is understood in the lighter sense. The core of the passage deals with the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' (you are forbidden to me like my mother). The ruling depends on the husband's intention: if he intends Zihar, it is Zihar; if he intends divorce (talaq), it is talaq. If he has no specific intention, Imam Abu Yusuf considers it Ila', while Imam Muhammad considers it Zihar. The text then examines a more explicit phrase: 'anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' (you are forbidden to me like my mother's back). Here, Abu Hanifa holds that it is exclusively Zihar, as the word 'kazahr ummi' is explicit for Zihar and cannot be used for anything else. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow it to be interpreted according to intention. Among the Sahibayn, an additional ikhtilaf arises: if the husband intends divorce, Muhammad says only divorce occurs (since after ba'in divorce Zihar is invalid), while Abu Yusuf says both divorce and Zihar occur. The passage concludes with a detailed presentation of each Imam's proof, rooted in linguistic principles (sarih vs. ambiguous, authoritative vs. probable) and the legal maxim that an authoritative term (muhkam) takes precedence over a probable one. The commentary explains each position clearly, reflecting the depth of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
ظهار (zihar): A pre-Islamic statement comparing one's wife to a female relative, which is prohibited and requires expiation.
إيلاء (ila'): An oath by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a specified period, with legal consequences.
صريح (sarih): Explicit, unambiguous term that requires no intention for its legal meaning.
محكم (muhkam): Authoritative term that is not subject to reinterpretation.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if a husband says 'anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' with no intention, the ruling is:
A) Ila'
B) Zihar
C) Talaq
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'ka'umi' without intention yields Ila' per Abu Yusuf and Zihar per Muhammad; with intention, it follows the intention.

Talaq — Divorce

The Exclusive Zihar of 'Kazahr Ummi' According to Imam Abu Hanifa
Explains the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on 'kazahr ummi' with sub-differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Abu Hanifa considers the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' exclusively Zihar, and state the contradictory views of the Sahibayn, including the sub-difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa: 'Kazahr ummi' is sarih for Zihar, leaving no room for other meanings.

The phrase 'anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' (you are forbidden to me like my mother's back) contains an explicit reference to Zihar through the word 'zahr' (back). This lesson examines the strongest intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds it is exclusively Zihar, while the Sahibayn allow interpretation by intention. Among them, an additional nuance arises regarding talaq.

Guided Path
1
State the phrase and the explicit element
The phrase includes 'kazahr ummi' (like my mother's back). The word 'zahr' is the specific anatomical reference used in the Quranic verse for Zihar.
Surah al-Mujadilah (58:2) explicitly mentions 'zahr' in the context of Zihar.
2
Present Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa rules that this phrase is exclusively Zihar, regardless of the husband's intention (even if he intends divorce or Ila). The term is sarih (explicit) and muhkam (authoritative).
Abu Hanifa's proof: 'Kazahr ummi' is unambiguous and cannot bear other meanings; the ambiguous 'haram' is referred to it.
3
Present the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the statement is interpreted according to the husband's intention because 'haram' is ambiguous and can include divorce, Ila, or Zihar.
The Sahibayn's proof: The phrase contains 'haram' which is general; intention specifies it.
4
Analyze the sub-ikhtilaf among the Sahibayn
If the husband intends talaq: Muhammad says only talaq occurs (Zihar is invalid after ba'in divorce); Abu Yusuf says both talaq and Zihar occur.
Muhammad: divorce effects baynunah, after which Zihar is impossible. Abu Yusuf: the explicit 'kazahr ummi' ensures Zihar, and talaq also occurs via intention.
5
Synthesize the proofs
Abu Hanifa: 'Kazahr ummi' is sarih, so it does not need intention and excludes other meanings. The Sahibayn: 'Haram' keeps it ambiguous, so intention matters. The disagreement stems from whether the phrase is considered sarih or not.
The principle: sarih terms override ambiguous ones according to Abu Hanifa.

ہو جائے گا۔
امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے انت علی حرام کہا اور طلاق کی نیت کی تو طلاق بائن واقع ہوگی اس کے بعد کظہر امی سے ظہار نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ بینونت کے بعد ظہار صحیح نہیں ہوتا۔ اور امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی اس کی نیت سے اور کنظبر می ، ظہار کے معنی میں صریح ہے۔ لہذا ی شخص ظہار کرنے والا بھی شمار ہو گا اور کلام کو اس کے ظاہر سے پھیرنے میں اس کی تصدیق نہیں کی جائے گی۔ امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ لفظ کظہر امی ظہار کے معنی میں صریح ہے۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ ظہار پر دلالت کرنے میں یہ لفظ نیت کا محتاج نہیں ہے پس اس لفظ میں ظہار کے علاوہ طلاق اور ایلاء کا احتمال نہیں ہو سکتا۔ پھر لفظ کظہر امی عدم احتمال غیر کی وجہ سے ظہار کے معنی میں محکم ہے اور انت علی حرام کئی معنی کا احتمال رکھتا ہے اور قاعدہ ہے کہ حمل کو محکم کی طرف پھیر دیا جاتا ہے اس وجہ سے انت علی حرام کو بھی ظہار کی طرف پھیر دیا جائے گا۔
Chapter of Zihar. Ashraf al-Hidaya Urdu commentary on Hidaya, volume 5. Ila's prohibitory effect is due to something external (ghayriha), meaning prohibition arises due to disrespecting Allah's name, while Zihar's prohibition is intrinsic (li-'aynihi) because Zihar is 'an abhorrent saying and a lie' (from the Quran). The outcome is that Ila's prohibition is lesser and lighter compared to Zihar's prohibition. And the principle is that if two prohibitions are combined in a single statement, that statement will be interpreted in accordance with the lesser prohibition. Therefore, when one says 'You are to me like my mother' with the intent of prohibition, it is interpreted as Ila, not Zihar. Imam Muhammad's proof is that in 'anti 'ala mithli ummi' there is the particle of comparison (harf al-tashbih), and the particle of comparison is specific to Zihar, so this statement is interpreted in the meaning of Zihar. And Allah knows best. A husband says to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' and intends Zihar or divorce; it is according to his intention. And if he says 'Anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' and intends Zihar or divorce, it is as he intended, because it can bear both meanings: Zihar due to the presence of comparison, and divorce due to the prohibition, and the comparison is a confirmation of the prohibition. If he has no intention, then according to Abu Yusuf it is Ila, and according to Muhammad it is Zihar. The two views have been explained. Commentary: If a husband says to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' and intends Zihar or divorce, then this statement is according to his intention. The proof is that this statement can bear both Zihar and divorce. Zihar because comparison is found, divorce because he has made her forbidden to himself, and in this case the comparison is a confirmation of the prohibition. If this person did not intend anything by his statement, then according to Imam Abu Yusuf this statement is Ila, and according to Imam Muhammad it is Zihar. The proofs of both have passed in the previous issue. A husband says to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' and intends divorce or Ila; is it Zihar or Ila? The statements of the jurists: If he says 'Anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' and intends by it divorce or Ila, it is not Zihar according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said it is as he intended, because prohibition can bear all of that as we have explained. However, according to Muhammad, if he intended divorce, it is not Zihar; according to Abu Yusuf, both occur. This has been mentioned in its place. Abu Hanifa's proof is that this statement is explicit (sarih) in the meaning of Zihar, so it cannot bear other than that. Then it is authoritative (muhkam), so this prohibition is referred to the prohibition of Zihar. Commentary: The issue is that a person said to his wife 'Anti 'alayya haram kazahr ummi' and intended divorce or Ila. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this statement is only Zihar, and Imam Ahmad holds the same. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is what he intended: if he intended Zihar, then Zihar; if divorce, then divorce; if Ila, then Ila. The proof of the two is that his statement 'Anti 'alayya haram' can bear each of the mentioned matters, and intention is valid for probability, so the ruling is according to his intention. However, there is a difference between the statements of the two: according to Imam Muhammad, if he intended divorce, only divorce occurs and this statement is not Zihar; according to Imam Abu Yusuf, this statement becomes both divorce and Zihar. Imam Muhammad's proof is that when the husband said 'Anti 'alayya haram' and intended divorce, a ba'in divorce occurs; after that, 'kazahr ummi' does not effect Zihar because Zihar is not valid after separation (baynunah). Imam Abu Yusuf's proof is that divorce occurs due to his intention, and 'kazahr ummi' is explicit in the meaning of Zihar, so this person is also considered a mazahir, and his word is not trusted when turning it from its apparent meaning. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is that the word 'kazahr ummi' is explicit in the meaning of Zihar; that is why it does not need intention in indicating Zihar. So this word cannot bear anything other than Zihar, such as divorce or Ila. Then the word 'kazahr ummi' is authoritative (muhkam) due to its lack of probability for other meanings, and 'Anti 'alayya haram' can bear several meanings. The principle is that interpretation is turned towards the authoritative, so 'Anti 'alayya haram' is also turned towards Zihar.

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya discusses the complex rulings surrounding ambiguous statements in the context of Zihar (a pre-Islamic practice prohibited in Islam that requires expiation). The passage first distinguishes between Ila' (an oath of abstinence) and Zihar, noting that Ila's prohibition is lighter because it is based on a secondary cause, whereas Zihar's prohibition is intrinsic. A key principle is established: when a statement can carry two different types of prohibition, it is understood in the lighter sense. The core of the passage deals with the phrase 'anti 'alayya haram ka'umi' (you are forbidden to me like my mother). If he has no specific intention, Imam Abu Yusuf considers it Ila', while Imam Muhammad considers it Zihar. Zihar Only from a Wife, Not from a Slave-Girl
Ruling with proof on Zihar's exclusivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that Zihar is exclusive to wives and identify the textual and analogical proofs for this ruling.

Core Idea
Zihar is valid only when directed at one's wife; it has no effect on slave-girls.

The Hanafi school establishes a crucial distinction in the rules of Zihar: it applies solely to a wife in a valid marriage contract. This lesson examines the passage from al-Jami' al-Saghir where Imam Muhammad clarifies that a statement of Zihar made to a slave-girl carries no legal consequences. Both direct Quranic evidence and analogy with the rules of divorce support this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (Zihar from a slave-girl) and its legal classification (batil – invalid).
Zihar is a specific legal pronouncement, not a general prohibition.
2
Locate the first dalil
Find the Quranic verse cited: 'Wa alladhina yuzahiruna minkum min nisa'ihim' (58:2). Note the specific wording 'min nisa'ihim' meaning 'from their wives'.
The verse uses the word 'nisa' which in this context refers to wives, not women in general.
3
Analyze wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the phrase 'min nisa'ihim' proves the ruling – it specifies wives, thus excluding all other women, including slave-girls.
This is a direct textual (nass) indication of exclusivity.
4
Locate the second dalil
Identify the qiyas (analogy): Zihar is derived from divorce (talaq); since talaq does not apply to slave-girls, Zihar also does not apply.
This analogy links Zihar to talaq in terms of legal scope.
5
Summarize the thamarah (practical consequence)
Conclude that a man who says a Zihar statement to his slave-girl is not a muzahir and incurs no kaffarah or legal effect.
This ruling prevents misuse of Zihar in non-marital relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And because permissibility in a slave-girl is derivative, so she cannot be attached to the married woman. And because Zihar is derived from divorce, and there is no divorce for a female slave. Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: Zihar (the prohibited statement of comparing one's wife to a female relative) is valid only when directed at one's wife (nikah). It is not valid when directed at a slave-girl (umm walad or otherwise). The proof is both textual and analogical. The textual proof is the Quranic verse 'alladhina yuzahiruna minkum min nisa'ihim' (those who pronounce Zihar among you from their wives), where 'nisa'ihim' refers specifically to wives. The analogical proof is twofold: (1) The permissibility (hull) in a slave-girl is derivative and does not reach the level of a fully married woman (mankuha). (2) Zihar is a concept derived from divorce (talaq), and slave-girls are not subject to divorce in the same way; they are subject to a different legal framework. Therefore, if a man says to his slave-girl a statement that would be Zihar if said to his wife, it has no legal effect as Zihar. The commentary emphasizes that this is the teaching of Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir, which is a foundational text of the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
زوجة (zawjah): A wife in a valid nikah (marriage contract).
أمة (ammah): A female slave, even if she is an umm walad (mother of her master's child).
مظاهر (muzahir): One who performs Zihar (declares his wife like a female relative).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It effects a divorce

Key Takeaway
Zihar is exclusive to a wife in a valid marriage; statements made to slave-girls have no legal effect as Zihar.
